      </section>

      <article class="post-1847 post type-post status-publish format-standard has-post-thumbnail category-web tag-api tag-mongodb tag-nodejs tag-passportjs tag-server entry" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/CreativeWork">
       <header class="entry-header">
        <h1 class="entry-title" itemprop="headline">RESTful API User Authentication with Node.js and AngularJS – Part 1/2: Server</h1> 
        <p class="entry-meta"><time class="entry-time" itemprop="datePublished" datetime="2015-11-17T19:00:05+00:00">November 17, 2015</time> By <span class="entry-author" itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><a href="https://devdactic.com/author/simon-reimler/" class="entry-author-link" itemprop="url" rel="author"><span class="entry-author-name" itemprop="name">Simon</span></a></span> <span class="entry-comments-link"><a href="https://devdactic.com/restful-api-user-authentication-1/#comments"><span class="dsq-postid" data-dsqidentifier="1847 http://devdactic.com/?p=1847">73 Comments</span></a></span> </p>
       </header>
       <div class="entry-content" itemprop="text">
        <img width="720" height="278" src="https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Restufl_user_authentication_API_backend.png" class="alignleft post-image entry-image" alt="RESTful API User Authentication with Node.js and AngularJS – Part 1/2: Server" itemprop="image" srcset="https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Restufl_user_authentication_API_backend.png 755w, https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Restufl_user_authentication_API_backend-300x116.png 300w" sizes="(max-width: 720px) 100vw, 720px">
        <p>My all time most viewed article is <a title="How To Handle User Authentication With AngularJS Inside Your Ionic App" href="http://devdactic.com/user-auth-angularjs-ionic/">How To Handle User Authentication With AngularJS Inside Your Ionic App</a>, and it’s also the one with the most questions. User authentication is one of the features almost every app(web and mobile) needs today. In this series I will show you my best practice for creating a <strong>RESTful API</strong> with user authentication. We will craft a little Node.js server which will allow us to sign up, authenticate and afterwards take request for protected endpoints. The second part will be how to build the frontend for such a REST backend.<br> <span id="more-1847"></span><br> <a href="http://devdactic.com/restful-api-user-authentication-2/">RESTful API User Authentication with Node.js and AngularJS – Part 2/2: Frontend</a></p> 
        <p>I use Node.js because it’s simple and straightforward, but you could obviously have any framework in the backend you like (or already have). The concept stays the same, just keep in mind that REST means <strong>stateless</strong> so we don’t want to have any kind of session. The authentication is handled by passing a token to the user after successful login, which will be sent to the server with every request from then.</p> 
        <p><script async id="_ck_58361" src="https://forms.convertkit.com/58361?v=5"></script></p> 
        <h3>Setting up our little Node.js backend</h3> 
        <p>Although I did not think about it, I used the complete <a href="http://mean.io/" onclick="_gaq.push(['_trackEvent', 'outbound-article', 'http://mean.io/', 'MEAN']);" title="MEAN" target="_blank">MEAN</a> stack for this series. This means (funny hu?):</p> 
        <ul> 
         <li><a href="https://www.mongodb.org/" onclick="_gaq.push(['_trackEvent', 'outbound-article', 'https://www.mongodb.org/', 'MongoDb']);" title="MongoDB" target="_blank">MongoDb</a> – Our Database</li> 
         <li><a href="http://expressjs.com/" onclick="_gaq.push(['_trackEvent', 'outbound-article', 'http://expressjs.com/', 'Expressjs']);" title="Expressjs" target="_blank">Expressjs</a> – A simple server</li> 
         <li><a href="https://angularjs.org/" onclick="_gaq.push(['_trackEvent', 'outbound-article', 'https://angularjs.org/', 'AngularJS']);" title="AngularJS" target="_blank">AngularJS</a> – For our frontend later</li> 
         <li><a href="https://nodejs.org" onclick="_gaq.push(['_trackEvent', 'outbound-article', 'https://nodejs.org', 'Node.js']);" title="Node.js" target="_blank">Node.js</a> – Our backend framework</li> 
        </ul> 
        <p><strong>So please make sure you have everything installed, especially the MongoDB </strong>(I already had everything but the DB).</p> 
        <p>The folder structure for our backend looks like this, so go ahead and create a folder and also create empty files:</p> 
        <p><a href="http://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_files.png"><img class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-1859" src="http://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_files.png" alt="rest_auth_backend_files" width="296" height="318" srcset="https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_files.png 296w, https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_files-279x300.png 279w" sizes="(max-width: 296px) 100vw, 296px"></a><br> I will come to what’s inside those files later, for now just have the structure ready.</p>

        <p>We will use <a href="http://passportjs.org/" onclick="_gaq.push(['_trackEvent', 'outbound-article', 'http://passportjs.org/', 'PassportJS']);" title="PassportJS" target="_blank">PassportJS</a> for our security and use the <a href="http://jwt.io/" onclick="_gaq.push(['_trackEvent', 'outbound-article', 'http://jwt.io/', 'JSON Web Token']);" title="JSON Web Token" target="_blank">JSON Web Token</a> idea for our token. This is a recommended approach for all RESTful APIs, the JWT is and will become more and more the standard.</p> 
        <p>Additionally we will install some encryption for passwords, everything the server needs and a simple connector to our MongoDB.</p>

                <span class="crayon-sy">.</span>
                <span class="crayon-v">comparePassword</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>

               </div>

                <span class="crayon-e">model</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">(</span>
                <span class="crayon-s">'User'</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">,</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-v">UserSchema</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">)</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">;</span>
               </div>
              </div></td> 
            </tr> 
           </tbody>
          </table> 
         </div> 
        </div>
        <p></p> 
        <p>So this might look a bit scary, but in general we only have a user with a unique name and a password as the schema for our DB. But we also don’t want to store the passwords in clear text, so whenever we want to save a user, we salt the password and store this value to our DB. This also means, we have to add a special compare method to compare those passwords, so we never get our hands on the real value of the user’s password!</p> 
        <p>Now the basics are set up, and you can start our server from now on just with:</p>

        <p>If you get the log your server is running, go to http://localhost:8080 and you should see our demo route!</p> 
        <h3>Creating our Strategy and User</h3> 
        <p>As I already mentioned, we use PassportJS which uses the term of <em>Strategy</em> for specific authentication behaviour. With PassportJS you can combine all kinds of logins (even social logins!) and assign that behviour to specific routes. Crazy shit.</p> 
        <p>But we just need a JWT strategy for our RESTful API, so go ahead and open the <strong>config/passport.js</strong> and insert:</p>

           </tbody>
          </table> 
         </div> 
        </div>
        <p></p> 
        <p><em>What this config does?</em><br> This code adds the JwtStrategy to our passport, and later you will se how to assign this specific strategy to a route. For now, this just defines how PassportJS tries to find a user with a given <strong>jwt_payload.id</strong>.</p> 
        <p>Next we need an endpoint to register new users. Inside our <strong>server.js</strong> add this code right below our dummy route:</p>

                <span class="crayon-v">apiRoutes</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">)</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">;</span>
               </div>
              </div></td> 
            </tr> 
           </tbody>
          </table> 
         </div> 
        </div>
        <p></p> 
        <p>We now use our MongoDB connection when we start the server, so if you get an error now this means your MongoDB might not be running or installed correctly!</p> 
        <p>We also call our created passport strategy with the passport instance we have, and create a bundle for our routes so we can all cal them under <strong>/api/*</strong> later on.</p> 
        <p>The route we created is our sign up, which needs a <strong>name</strong> and <strong>password</strong> field. If those are given, we try to create a new user (where the password will be encrypted!) and save that user. If this is successful, we can report great success to the user!</p> 
        <p>At this point I encourage you to use <a href="https://chrome.google.com/webstore/detail/postman/fhbjgbiflinjbdggehcddcbncdddomop" onclick="_gaq.push(['_trackEvent', 'outbound-article', 'https://chrome.google.com/webstore/detail/postman/fhbjgbiflinjbdggehcddcbncdddomop', 'Postman']);" title="Postman" target="_blank">Postman</a>, an awesome tool to play around with your REST API. The request inside Postman would now look like this, including already the response after success:<br> <a href="http://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/node_backend_postman_signup.png"><img class="aligncenter size-large wp-image-1863" src="http://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/node_backend_postman_signup-1024x441.png" alt="node_backend_postman_signup" width="1000" height="431" srcset="https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/node_backend_postman_signup-1024x441.png 1024w, https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/node_backend_postman_signup-300x129.png 300w, https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/node_backend_postman_signup-1087x468.png 1087w" sizes="(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px"></a><br> I also used <a href="http://robomongo.org/" onclick="_gaq.push(['_trackEvent', 'outbound-article', 'http://robomongo.org/', 'Robomongo']);" title="Robomongo" target="_blank">Robomongo</a> to inspect my MongoDB and to see the created user object, but this tool is more or less optional.</p> 
        <h3>User Authentication with JSON Web Token</h3> 
        <p>We got our server, the strategy, the database model, the signup… now comes the fun!</p> 
        <p>As I said before, we want to authenticate a user and in return give him a token, which he should include in all further request. Let’s start with the authentication, so in your <strong>server.js</strong> add this right after our signup route:</p>

           </tbody>
          </table> 
         </div> 
        </div>
        <p></p> 
        <p>There is really not much we have to do at this point, as this route needs to be unprotected. We just try to identify this user by finding him in our DB (remember the name was unique) and afterwards we use our compare function to check if the send token matches the stored&amp;encrypted password.</p> 
        <p>If all of this was a success, we create a new JWT with the user object and encode it with our secret. This token will be returned to the user in the form of “JWT ” because PassportJS expected the token to look like this. Don’t kill the messenger. Take this image of postman as an excuse:</p> 
        <p><a href="http://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_auth.png"><img class="aligncenter size-large wp-image-1865" src="http://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_auth-1024x481.png" alt="rest_auth_backend_auth" width="1000" height="470" srcset="https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_auth-1024x481.png 1024w, https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_auth-300x141.png 300w, https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_auth-1087x511.png 1087w" sizes="(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px"></a></p> 
        <p>The last part now is to have some sort of protected route, so right below our authentication add this protected endpoint:</p> 
        <p></p>

               <div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-59beafae0981c372541960-28">
                <span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span>
                <span class="crayon-st">if</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">(</span>
                <span class="crayon-v">parted</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">.</span>
                <span class="crayon-v">length</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-o">===</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-cn">2</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">)</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">{</span>
               </div>
               <div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-59beafae0981c372541960-29">
                <span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span>
                <span class="crayon-st">return</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-v">parted</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">[</span>
                <span class="crayon-cn">1</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">]</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">;</span>
               </div>
               <div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-59beafae0981c372541960-30">
                <span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">}</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-st">else</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">{</span>
               </div>
               <div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-59beafae0981c372541960-31">
                <span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span>
                <span class="crayon-st">return</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-t">null</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">;</span>
               </div>
               <div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-59beafae0981c372541960-32">
                <span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">}</span>
               </div>
               <div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-59beafae0981c372541960-33">
                <span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">}</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-st">else</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">{</span>
               </div>
               <div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-59beafae0981c372541960-34">
                <span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span>
                <span class="crayon-st">return</span>
                <span class="crayon-h"> </span>
                <span class="crayon-t">null</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">;</span>
               </div>
               <div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-59beafae0981c372541960-35">
                <span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">}</span>
               </div>
               <div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-59beafae0981c372541960-36">
                <span class="crayon-sy">}</span>
                <span class="crayon-sy">;</span>
               </div>
              </div></td> 
            </tr> 
           </tbody>
          </table> 
         </div> 
        </div>
        <p></p> 
        <p>First of all, we assign our passport with the jwt strategy to this route. This means, before any of our code inside is executed, PassportJS will check if this user is allowed to see this route (by using the created strategy).</p> 
        <p>Afterwards, we need to extract the data from the JWT with mighty split function and decode our token. We can then find our user and send out a special greeting with his name to see that we actually found the right information and the user is in general allowed to see this.</p> 
        <p>This is just some dummy shit, but I think you get the point of how to handle things. The result would look like this:<br> <a href="http://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_protected.png"><img class="aligncenter size-large wp-image-1866" src="http://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_protected-1024x399.png" alt="rest_auth_backend_protected" width="1000" height="390" srcset="https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_protected-1024x399.png 1024w, https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_protected-300x117.png 300w, https://devdactic.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/rest_auth_backend_protected-1087x424.png 1087w" sizes="(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px"></a></p> 
        <h3>Conclusion</h3> 
        <p>Implementing a RESTful API with user authentication is not that hard if you know how to do it. Authentication via JWT is a great and my recommended way, because it’s really easy to use and also very powerful. In the next part of this series I will show how to communicate with this backend properly using AngularJS inside an Ionic app (the concept and most of the code is plain AngularJS, if you don’t want it mobile).</p> 
        <div class="box">
         Checkout the second part now:
         <br> 
         <a href="http://devdactic.com/restful-api-user-authentication-2/">RESTful API User Authentication with Node.js and AngularJS – Part 2/2: Frontend</a>
        </div> 
        <p>Happy Coding,<br> Simon</p>

            <ul class="children"> 
             <li class="comment odd alt depth-3" id="dsq-comment-1434"> 
              <div id="dsq-comment-header-1434" class="dsq-comment-header"> 
               <cite id="dsq-cite-1434"> <span id="dsq-author-user-1434">Alejandro Ventura</span> </cite> 
              </div> 
              <div id="dsq-comment-body-1434" class="dsq-comment-body"> 
               <div id="dsq-comment-message-1434" class="dsq-comment-message">
                <p>Well I’m working on a project but I’m only in charge of the backend side so I provide an API written in SailsJS and I need to make a logout route available for the android app and fronted app clients so they finish the session manually.</p> 
                <p>Also, I already send a token to them but I want the user only could be signed in once at a time from the mobile app or web app. So when they get logged in I need to delete the previous generated token in order to only use the newest one.</p> 
                <p>Any idea how to do that? Thanks in advance!</p> 
               </div> 
              </div> 
              <ul class="children"> 
               <li class="comment even depth-4" id="dsq-comment-1441"> 
                <div id="dsq-comment-header-1441" class="dsq-comment-header"> 
                 <cite id="dsq-cite-1441"> <a id="dsq-author-user-1441" href="http://devdactic.com/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Simon Reimler</a> </cite> 
                </div> 
                <div id="dsq-comment-body-1441" class="dsq-comment-body"> 
                 <div id="dsq-comment-message-1441" class="dsq-comment-message">
                  <p>Well from the backend you can’t really delete the frontend token, the only solution that comes to my mind:</p> 
                  <p>When you send the token to the frontend after authentication, store that token in a DB with “valid” tokens. Then you would also have to check this database on every request to see if this token is still valid.</p> 
                  <p>On logout you can then remove the token or invalidate it, so that even if the frontend might still have the token, it’s no longer valid.</p> 
                  <p>Hope this helps or give’s you an idea!</p> 
                 </div> 
                </div> 
                <ul class="children"> 
                 <li class="comment odd alt depth-5" id="dsq-comment-1476"> 
                  <div id="dsq-comment-header-1476" class="dsq-comment-header"> 
                   <cite id="dsq-cite-1476"> <span id="dsq-author-user-1476">Alejandro Ventura</span> </cite> 
                  </div> 
                  <div id="dsq-comment-body-1476" class="dsq-comment-body"> 
                   <div id="dsq-comment-message-1476" class="dsq-comment-message">
                    <p>Alright I its pretty much similar to the idea I have made.</p> 
                    <p>First:<br> I take the createdAt token’s value and store in the Users table as the last_login attribute.</p> 
                    <p>Second:<br> On every request I see the creation date of the incoming token and if it is older than the last_login attribute, I just deny access.</p> 
                    <p>Advantage:<br> I will always have only one user logged in, it is the last generated token.<br> No new table needed in the DB just a new field for every User.</p> 
                   </div> 
                  </div> </li>

            <p>One minor issue. I don’t see any reason to pass back the password in the token. Sure, to even decode the token you need to know the secret used to encode it, and even then the password itself is encrypted. But nevertheless, rather than encode the user object into the token, why not just encode the username and ID, leaving the password out of it. Here you can see the full user object when I decode the token.</p>

         <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="dsq-comment-1715"> 
          <div id="dsq-comment-header-1715" class="dsq-comment-header"> 
           <cite id="dsq-cite-1715"> <span id="dsq-author-user-1715">Rob Louie</span> </cite> 
          </div> 
          <div id="dsq-comment-body-1715" class="dsq-comment-body"> 
           <div id="dsq-comment-message-1715" class="dsq-comment-message">
            <p>Hey Simon, thanks responding so quickly to my previous post. I like that you’re actually active on here. So, I’ve been working with Passport a bit more refining what I learned from your blog post and I have to say…all the code in the memberinfo protected endpoint is redundant.</p> 
            <p>You already told the JWT strategy to look up your user by the id in the token in the passport.js file. I mean, the whole point of using Passport and setting up the verify callback in the strategy is to handle that for you. Whenever you hit that endpoint with a token Passport will get the ID from the token and search the database for a user with that ID. Provided it can decrypt the token and find the user all is fine, but if that is not the case Passport will return Unauthorized.</p> 
            <p> So in the memberinfo callback you don’t need to check if the token doesn’t exist, because at this point if there was no token Passport would not have let you get to the endpoint. And you don’t need to check if the user exists, because if it doesn’t then Passport won’t let you get to the endpoint. You don’t need to decrypt the token, because Passport already did. And you don’t need to hit the database again to get the user, because Passport already did that for you and put the user on to the request. That was all already set up in the passport.js file. Literally all that needs to be in the memberinfo callback is this one line:<br> res.json({success: true, msg: ‘Welcome in the member area ‘ + req.user.username + ‘!’});</p> 
           </div> 
          </div> 
          <ul class="children"> 
           <li class="comment odd alt depth-2" id="dsq-comment-2170">

            <p>hey, nice article . it helped me to kick start but i believe you didnt do it right .</p> 
            <p>Should have been as follows:</p> 
            <p>in passport.js:</p> 
            <p>/**</p> 
            <p> * Created by angelnethaniel on 20/05/2016.</p> 
            <p> */</p> 
            <p>var JwtStrategy = require(‘passport-jwt’).Strategy;</p> 
            <p>// load up the user model</p> 
            <p>var User = require(‘../app/models/user’);</p> 
            <p>var config = require(‘../config/database’); // get db config file</p> 
            <p>module.exports = function(passport) {</p> 
            <p> var opts = {};</p> 
            <p> opts.secretOrKey = config.secret;</p> 
            <p> passport.use(new JwtStrategy(opts, function(jwt_payload, done) {</p> 
            <p> User.findOne({_id: jwt_payload._id}, function(err, user) {</p> 
            <p> if (err) {</p> 
            <p> return done(err, false);</p> 
            <p> }</p> 
            <p> if (user) {</p> 
            <p> done(null, user);</p> 
            <p> } else {</p> 
            <p> done(null, false);</p> 
            <p> }</p> 
            <p> });</p> 
            <p> }));</p> 
            <p>};</p> 
            <p>in the api like that :</p> 
            <p>apiRoutes.get(‘/memberinfo’, passport.authenticate(‘jwt’, { session: false}), function(req, res) {</p> 
            <p> res.send(req.user);<br> }</p>

           <li class="comment odd alt depth-2" id="dsq-comment-2557"> 
            <div id="dsq-comment-header-2557" class="dsq-comment-header"> 
             <cite id="dsq-cite-2557"> <a id="dsq-author-user-2557" href="http://linnertmedia.de/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Andreas Linnert</a> </cite> 
            </div> 
            <div id="dsq-comment-body-2557" class="dsq-comment-body"> 
             <div id="dsq-comment-message-2557" class="dsq-comment-message">
              <p>I needed some time to remember and figure it out but now I got it. Decoding the token in the route function really isn’t necessary. We already obtain the user object from the database in <code>config/passport.js</code>. This user object is accessible via <code>req.user</code>.<br> So the ‘/memberinfo’ function actually is a one-liner. response.json() is enough. But <code>user.name</code> gets <code>req.user.name</code>.<br> Now I just wonder how you can customize error responses. Currently if you send a wrong JWT the response is just “Unauthorized” (no JSON, status 401).<br> Thanks for the question. This makes it a lot clearer to me too.</p> 
             </div> 
            </div> </li>

           <cite id="dsq-cite-2877"> <span id="dsq-author-user-2877">Flibo</span> </cite> 
          </div> 
          <div id="dsq-comment-body-2877" class="dsq-comment-body"> 
           <div id="dsq-comment-message-2877" class="dsq-comment-message">
            <p>Thanks for the super helpful article. However, I think when you decode the JWT in /memberinfo, it’s an error to use findOne with ‘name’ as the argument. This way if you’re logged in as ‘test’, and there is a user with name ‘test two’, user ‘test’ will be shown the user info of ‘test two’. You can easily circumvent this by using findById(decoded._id) instead.</p> 
           </div> 
          </div> </li>

				jQuery(window).on( 'load', function(){
					startclock();
				});
				function stopclock (){
				  if(timerRunning) clearTimeout(timerID);
				  timerRunning = false;
				  document.cookie="time=0";
				}
				function showtime () {
				  var now = new Date();
				  var my = now.getTime() ;
				  now = new Date(my-diffms) ;
				  document.cookie="time="+now.toLocaleString();
				  timerID = setTimeout('showtime()',10000);
				  timerRunning = true;
				}
				function startclock () {
				  stopclock();
				  showtime();
				}
